Jensen Huang calls the AI jobs panic ‘complete nonsense’, and takes aim at his peers
2026-07-23

Jensen Huang calls the AI jobs panic ‘complete nonsense’, and takes aim at his peers

The Next Web | 2026-07-23

Visit Original Website
Jensen Huang has heard the warnings. He is not buying them. The Nvidia chief runs the world’s most valuable company on the back of the AI boom. So when he calls the darkest predictions about that boom “complete nonsense,” it is worth asking whether he is right, or just talking his book. In a wide-ranging Axios interview with Mike Allen, Huang took on two claims at once. That AI will end humanity, and that it will wipe out half of American jobs. Both are “complete nonsense,” he said, and “all of the evidence points exactly to the opposite.” His real argument is subtler than the soundbite. It is not that no jobs change. It is that people keep confusing a task with a job. The line is one Huang says he gives to young people. The latest rumblings from the EU tech scene, a story from our wise ol' founder Boris, and some questionable AI art. It's free, every week, in your inbox. Sign up now! “Don’t mistake your task for the job.” A radiologist’s task is reading scans, and AI can do much of that. But the job, he argues, is ending suffering and guiding patients. That does not vanish when the scan-reading is automated. Some roles will still go. Call-centre work, he granted, is “very likely to be highly automated.” The judgement around it, he thinks, stays human. He came with numbers. The number of radiologists is rising, by about 20%, he said, because AI lets each one see more patients, a claim others have backed. Paralegals are up around 10%. Manufacturing jobs have grown some 50% in recent years, he claimed, as the industry races to build data centres. His reframe of the fear is blunt. “AI is not going to destroy all of our jobs,” he said. “Someone who uses AI is going to take your jobs.” Asked why Asia is so much sunnier about AI, Huang said the doomers “spend too much time theorizing about these science fiction outcomes,” which “makes them sound smart.” Pressed on whether he meant fellow AI bosses, he softened. The aim was clear enough. Talk of a singularity, of a simulation, of machine consciousness, is “all made up.” “It’s fine to warn people,” he added. “It’s absolutely inappropriate to make things up.” He took the same message to Washington. AI is not a “100-metre dash” with a single winner, he said, and policymakers risk over-correcting if they “fall for these narratives.” His advice to the Trump administration was to talk to many scientists, not one or two. He even waved off the idea of the government taking a stake in Nvidia as “unnecessary.” Huang’s brighter vision is expansive. He expects a trillion AI agents running at once. He says the “ChatGPT moment” for robots has already arrived, with useful machines perhaps three to four years away. An AI bubble “will come someday,” he allowed, “just not today,” and probably not within five years. It is a bracing counter-story, and Huang has every reason to tell it, because more AI means more Nvidia chips. The job figures are his own framing, and rivals such as Anthropic’s Dario Amodei still forecast the opposite. Yet his central distinction is hard to wave away. Automation has reshaped work before without ending it, and “someone who uses AI is going to take your jobs” is a more honest worry than a robot uprising. The real question is whether people believe him before they act on the other one. Get the most important tech news in your inbox each week.

Apple spent years insisting the Mac would never get a touchscreen. That is about to change. The refresh spans this autumn and next year, and covers roughly 11 machines, Bloomberg’s Mark Gurman reported. It starts modestly, with a refreshed entry 14-inch MacBook Pro on the new M6 chip and the first new iMacs in two years. Both are due this autumn. A representative for Apple declined to comment. People running compute-heavy AI agents have taken to the Mac mini and Mac Studio, prizing their power and their knack for hooking into other systems. Demand has outrun supply so badly that some new orders will not ship for three months. That pull is reshaping Apple’s plans. New Mac mini and Mac Studio models are in testing, with upgraded chips, but their timing hinges on a shortage of memory chips. A new MacBook Air lands early next year, with an OLED version pencilled in for as early as 2028. A base MacBook Pro with a standard M7 chip follows, as does a refreshed MacBook Neo, Apple’s $699 budget laptop. Further out sits an OLED iMac, matching the screen tech arriving elsewhere in the range. The prices tell their own story. Apple lifted Mac prices across the board in June, and a fully loaded 16-inch MacBook Pro now runs to $9,999. A device-leasing plan that includes the Mac starts on 28 July. Apple built its fortune on phones. But the Mac, of all things, is having an AI moment, and the touchscreen it swore it would never make is the proof. OpenAI plans a $30bn Georgia data centre, and a charm offensive to match
2026-07-23

OpenAI plans a $30bn Georgia data centre, and a charm offensive to match

The Next Web | 2026-07-23

Visit Original Website
Building a data centre used to be a quiet affair. Not any more. OpenAI’s plan for a giant new site in Georgia arrives with a charm offensive attached, because the industry has learned that the hard part is no longer the concrete. It is the neighbours. The company will spend tens of billions of dollars on a complex near Savannah, Bloomberg reported. It has contracted with Georgia Power for 3.2 gigawatts of electricity in Effingham County. A single gigawatt is enough to run about 750,000 homes. Codenamed Project Camellia, the site would rank among OpenAI’s largest, DataCenterDynamics reported. The figures are staggered. OpenAI has committed $20bn in the county to qualify for an incentive package due for a vote this week. At the full 3.2GW, the bill would top $30bn, said Sachin Katti, its vice-president of compute strategy. Several hundred megawatts come online from 2028, the rest by 2032. The company is hunting for partners to help pay for and build it. The more interesting shift is who now holds the pen. OpenAI is no longer just renting these places. It is designing them, and it wants to give the designs away. Stargate, OpenAI’s infrastructure programme, began as a joint venture with Oracle and SoftBank. Now the partners vary, and OpenAI is leading the planning itself. “There’s a bunch of lessons around how these things should be designed,” Katti said, that “cut costs as well as make it faster to build.” The company plans to open-source its blueprints for data centres, hardware, and networking. It is echoing the Open Compute Project that Meta started in 2011. This is not blanket expansion. OpenAI has recently paused projects in the UK and Norway, and declined to grow its flagship Texas campus, trimming spending as it eyes a stock-market debut. Rivals keep pushing. Anthropic has leased more than 300MW from SpaceX’s Colossus 1 in Memphis, and Meta has committed over $250bn to a single Louisiana campus. The pitch to locals is the tell. Data centres now draw fierce local opposition over power prices and water. So OpenAI says it will pay the full cost of the electrical work, which state rules bar it from passing to existing customers, and it will throttle power at peak times. It promises a closed-loop cooling system, hundreds of millions in taxes, a community fund, and AI credits for local students. It will also listen. The company met county officials and starts talking to residents on Thursday, said global affairs chief Chris Lehane, and it will commission an independent audit of its promises. “All issues are going to be local,” he said. Whether that is enough to keep a 3.2-gigawatt machine welcome in a Georgia county is the question the next few years will answer. Get the most important tech news in your inbox each week.

A retina chip that restores sight is going on sale in Europe
2026-07-23

A retina chip that restores sight is going on sale in Europe

The Next Web | 2026-07-23

Visit Original Website
Geographic atrophy is the worst form of age-related macular degeneration. For most people who have it, the centre of the world slowly greys out. Faces blur. Words vanish. There is no cure, only treatments that slow the loss. Now a small chip is giving some of that sight back. Science Corp, based in Alameda, California, will begin selling its Prima implant in Europe, the company said. The device is a chip that sits at the back of the eye and works with a special pair of glasses. The glasses beam near-infrared light to the chip, which converts it into signals for the retina. A “zoom” feature lets patients magnify letters. Prima has earned a CE mark from the notified body DEKRA, Bloomberg reported, clearing it for sale across 30 European countries. The milestone is not only medical. This is the first device from a US brain-implant company to reach patients widely. The firm that got there is not the famous one. Science is the second most valuable US brain-implant company, behind Elon Musk’s Neuralink. Its founder, Max Hodak, helped start Neuralink before leaving to build this. TNW City Coworking space - Where your best work happens A workspace designed for growth, collaboration, and endless networking opportunities in the heart of tech. “This is the first in hopefully a long series of commercial products that proceed to advance that horizon, sometimes incrementally, but relentlessly,” Hodak said in an interview. The word that matters there is commercial. Brain-implant companies have run trials for years. Selling a device to ordinary patients is a different threshold. The trial behind the approval was small but striking. Published in the New England Journal of Medicine, it followed 38 patients across 17 sites in five countries. On average they gained 25.5 letters, more than five lines on a standard eye chart. And 84% could again read letters, numbers, and words. It is not a cure. Geographic atrophy from AMD affects more than five million people worldwide. But it is a genuine return of function. Science has raised $489m at a $1.5bn valuation. The geography is telling. Science expects its first commercial patient in August, probably in Germany, then dozens more this year and around 200 next. The US comes later. The company is working with the Food and Drug Administration and hopes to reach the US market next year. Prima already holds FDA Breakthrough Device and Humanitarian Use Device designations, both meant to speed narrow, high-need treatments. Meanwhile China has named brain tech a national priority and cleared its own first implant. The global race is drawing in billions. The science that restores sight is arriving. Where you can buy it, and what it will cost, is the next question. For now the answer is simple: Europe, price unset. Get the most important tech news in your inbox each week.
